        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.9b17952"><meta property="og:title" content="Chalcopyrite-Derived NaxMO2 (M = Cu, Fe, Mn) Cathode: Tuning Impurities for Self-Doping"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/asset/images/acsami.9b17952.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Discovering cathode materials composed of earth-abundant elements has become the current priority for developing sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) to meet the ever-increasing demand of large-scale energy storage. Herein, for the first time, layered NaxMO2 (M = Cu, Fe, Mn) cathodes are successfully prepared by directly using concentrated chalcopyrite ores as precursors. Greatly, impurity elements like Si and Ca are found to be crucial to tailoring the phase structure of as-obtained layered oxides as a P2 or O3 type, which removes the traditional concern that the impurities may restrict the utilization of natural ores. More interestingly, a certain amount of the Ca elements remaining in the Na sites through a self-doping process endows the P2-type products with enhanced structural stability. In half-cells, P2-type NaxMO2 with self-doped Ca elements shows superior rate capability and cycling stability (56 mAh g–1 at 5 C and 90% capacity retention after 100 cycles at 1 C). In contrast, less impurity elements are favorable for O3-type oxides to achieve a high capacity of 107 mAh g–1 at 0.1 C and 84% capacity retention after 200 cycles at 2 C. This new strategy would efficiently shorten the process for preparing electrode materials and open a feasible route to construct cheap and durable SIBs."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/asset/images/acsami.9b17952.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="93188a6559dd54b8-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="93188a6559dd54b8-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '9aafd44d-94a8-4c83-99a1-6ff026d196f2'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="93188a6559dd54b8-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

<script nonce="93188a6559dd54b8-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Chalcopyrite-Derived Na&lt;italic toggle&#x3D;&quot;yes&quot;&gt;&lt;sub&gt;x&lt;/sub&gt;&lt;/italic&gt;MO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; (M &#x3D; Cu, Fe, Mn) Cathode: Tuning Impurities for Self-Doping", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.9b17952", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Chalcopyrite-Derived Na&lt;italic toggle&#x3D;&quot;yes&quot;&gt;&lt;sub&gt;x&lt;/sub&gt;&lt;/italic&gt;MO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; (M &#x3D; Cu, Fe, Mn) Cathode: Tuning Impurities for Self-Doping", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.9b17952", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Chalcopyrite-Derived Na&lt;italic toggle&#x3D;&quot;yes&quot;&gt;&lt;sub&gt;x&lt;/sub&gt;&lt;/italic&gt;MO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; (M &#x3D; Cu, Fe, Mn) Cathode: Tuning Impurities for Self-Doping", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.9b17952", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Chalcopyrite-Derived Na&lt;italic toggle&#x3D;&quot;yes&quot;&gt;&lt;sub&gt;x&lt;/sub&gt;&lt;/italic&gt;MO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; (M &#x3D; Cu, Fe, Mn) Cathode: Tuning Impurities for Self-Doping", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.9b17952", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Chalcopyrite-Derived Na&lt;italic toggle&#x3D;&quot;yes&quot;&gt;&lt;sub&gt;x&lt;/sub&gt;&lt;/italic&gt;MO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; (M &#x3D; Cu, Fe, Mn) Cathode: Tuning Impurities for Self-Doping", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.9b17952", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Chalcopyrite-Derived Na&lt;italic toggle&#x3D;&quot;yes&quot;&gt;&lt;sub&gt;x&lt;/sub&gt;&lt;/italic&gt;MO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; (M &#x3D; Cu, Fe, Mn) Cathode: Tuning Impurities for Self-Doping", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.9b17952", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Chalcopyrite-Derived Na&lt;italic toggle&#x3D;&quot;yes&quot;&gt;&lt;sub&gt;x&lt;/sub&gt;&lt;/italic&gt;MO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; (M &#x3D; Cu, Fe, Mn) Cathode: Tuning Impurities for Self-Doping", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.9b17952", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Chalcopyrite-Derived Na&lt;italic toggle&#x3D;&quot;yes&quot;&gt;&lt;sub&gt;x&lt;/sub&gt;&lt;/italic&gt;MO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; (M &#x3D; Cu, Fe, Mn) Cathode: Tuning Impurities for Self-Doping", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.9b17952", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Chalcopyrite-Derived Na&lt;italic toggle&#x3D;&quot;yes&quot;&gt;&lt;sub&gt;x&lt;/sub&gt;&lt;/italic&gt;MO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; (M &#x3D; Cu, Fe, Mn) Cathode: Tuning Impurities for Self-Doping", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.9b17952", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});

Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/asset/images/medium/am9b17952_0001.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/asset/images/large/am9b17952_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b17952&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Discovering cathode materials composed of earth-abundant elements has become the current priority for developing sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) to meet the ever-increasing demand of large-scale energy storage. Herein, for the first time, layered Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>MO<sub>2</sub> (M = Cu, Fe, Mn) cathodes are successfully prepared by directly using concentrated chalcopyrite ores as precursors. Greatly, impurity elements like Si and Ca are found to be crucial to tailoring the phase structure of as-obtained layered oxides as a P2 or O3 type, which removes the traditional concern that the impurities may restrict the utilization of natural ores. More interestingly, a certain amount of the Ca elements remaining in the Na sites through a self-doping process endows the P2-type products with enhanced structural stability. In half-cells, P2-type Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>MO<sub>2</sub> with self-doped Ca elements shows superior rate capability and cycling stability (56 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 5 C and 90% capacity retention after 100 cycles at 1 C). In contrast, less impurity elements are favorable for O3-type oxides to achieve a high capacity of 107 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1 C and 84% capacity retention after 200 cycles at 2 C. This new strategy would efficiently shorten the process for preparing electrode materials and open a feasible route to construct cheap and durable SIBs.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D2432%26pageCount%3D13%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DXu%2BGao%252C%2BFeng%2BJiang%252C%2BYue%2BYang%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D12%26issueNum%3D2%26contentID%3Dacsami.9b17952%26title%3DChalcopyrite-Derived%2BNaxMO2%2B%2528M%2B%253D%2BCu%252C%2BFe%252C%2BMn%2529%2BCathode%253A%2BTuning%2BImpurities%2Bfor%2BSelf-Doping%26numPages%3D13%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D2444%26publicationDate%3DJanuary%2B2020" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2019 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes"> Electrodes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290692&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Elements"> Elements </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292506&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Materials"> Materials </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290689&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Oxides"> Oxides </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292316&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Transition metals"> Transition metals </a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Battery&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20battery">sodium-ion battery</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Cathode+Material&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_cathode%20material">cathode material</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Chalcopyrite&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_chalcopyrite">chalcopyrite</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Layered+Oxide&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_layered%20oxide">layered oxide</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Self-doping&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_self-doping">self-doping</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Over the past decades, climate change, environment pollution, and energy crisis issues have led to a rapid development of renewable and clean energy sources such as hydroelectricity, wind, and solar power. The common drawback of these powers is their intermittent character, which calls for suitable electricity storage facilities with low costs, high power, and long lifetimes to balance supply and demand in real time. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3" aria-label="reference 1 to 3">(1−3)</a> Although lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have achieved great commercial success in portable electronics and electrical vehicle applications owing to high energy density, they lose superiority in the field of large-scale energy storage due to the limited resources and increasing price of lithium. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref5 ref6" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref4 ref5 ref6" aria-label="reference 4 to 6">(4−6)</a> In this case, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) attract worldwide attention again in light of the natural abundance of sodium resources and similar intercalation chemistry of sodium ions to their lithium counterparts. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12" aria-label="reference 7 to 12">(7−12)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Theoretically speaking, SIBs may not reach the energy density of the LIBs when considering that Na is more than three times heavier than Li and the standard electrochemical potential of Na<sup>+</sup>/Na (−2.71 V) is higher than Li<sup>+</sup>/Li (−3.04 V) with respect to the standard hydrogen electrode (SHE). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13" aria-label="reference 13">(13)</a> For this reason, the development of SIBs has been focused on further reducing the production cost to ensure the competitiveness. One of the main challenges is the exploration of new cathode materials with cheap and abundant elements since the prices of Co, Ni, and V have become more expensive than before. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref14" aria-label="reference 14">(14)</a> Among numerous transition-metal elements, Fe and Mn seem to hold great potential because of their geographical distributions and outstanding electrochemical activities in many types of cathode systems like layered/tunnel oxides, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18" aria-label="reference 15 to 18">(15−18)</a> phosphates, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19 ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref19 ref20" aria-label="reference 19 and 20">(19,20)</a> and Prussian blue analogues (PBAs). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24" aria-label="reference 21 to 24">(21−24)</a> In terms of the specific capacity, Fe–Mn-based oxides are much better than the others, but their development is seriously constrained by poor structural stabilities. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref25 ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref15 ref25 ref26" aria-label="reference 15, 25 and 26">(15,25,26)</a> To solve this problem, different types of cations (e.g., Li<sup>+</sup>, Mg<sup>2+</sup>, Cu<sup>2+</sup>, Al<sup>3+</sup>, and Ti<sup>4+</sup>) were induced into the host materials, replacing Fe or Mn, in order to modulate the energy level or surface structure. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 27 to 31">(27−31)</a> Notably, the Cu element was found to be electrochemically active between bivalence and trivalence in layered oxides, greatly improving the working potential and structural stability. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35" aria-label="reference 32 to 35">(32−35)</a> This discovery is quite attractive since Cu is also an abundant element, which opens the way to develop cheap cathodes based on Cu, Fe, and Mn elements.</div><div class="NLM_p">Compared to the wide concern on the compositions of positive-electrode materials, much less attention is given to the long and complicated chemical conversion processes from natural resources to these cathode materials. Generally, the oxide-type cathode materials are obtained through a solid-state reaction process using metal oxides with high purity as ingredients. Noteworthy, these metal oxides are produced by chemosynthesis or electrolytic routes. In other words, a considerable amount of energy is consumed to obtain these high-purity raw materials from natural ore resources. It is proposed that, if the natural ores can be directly used to prepare electrode materials, the energy consumption and cost would be further economized. Not surprisingly, the impurities in natural ores would be the biggest issue, but if the main impurity elements are favorable or at least harmless to the final products, they seem to be acceptable. As mentioned above, cation doping is one of the normal strategies to optimize the structure and improve the electrochemical performance of cathode materials. That is to say, the impurity elements do not necessarily mean negative effects. Revealing the specific effects of these impurity elements may enable the design of self-doping layered oxides based on natural ores. This assumption enlightened us to take a fresh look at the natural ore materials like chalcopyrite. As the most abundant copper mineral, the chalcopyrite is the main source of copper for extractive metallurgy. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36 ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36 ref37" aria-label="reference 36 and 37">(36,37)</a> The chemical formula of chalcopyrite is written as CuFeS<sub>2</sub>, indicating that it can provide both Cu and Fe simultaneously. Most importantly, the natural chalcopyrite can be purified to a high degree through a typical mineral-concentrating process consisting of crushing, ball-milling, and flotation. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38 ref39 ref40 ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38 ref39 ref40 ref41" aria-label="reference 38 to 41">(38−41)</a> Hence, the key point is to find a suitable synthesis method and discover the influences of the impurity elements.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Based on the discussions above, herein, P2- and O3-type layered oxides (Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>MO<sub>2</sub>, M = Cu, Fe, Mn) are successfully prepared through a solid-state reaction process using chalcopyrite as the raw material. The main impurity elements beyond the theoretical composition are found to be Si and Ca. Combining inductively coupled plasma–atomic emission spectroscopy (ICP-AES), X-ray diffraction (XRD), and Rietveld refinement methods, the content of the impurity elements is evidenced to have great impact on the amount of Na in a unit cell and the phase structure of the products. With the decrease of impurity elements, the amount of Na atoms entering into the lattice can be increased, leading to a change of the phase structure from P2-type to O3-type. When used as the cathode materials for SIBs, both of the P2- and O3-type layered oxides derived from chalcopyrite exhibit comparable rates and cyclic performances to the materials obtained with high-purity ingredients. Noteworthy, the existence of a small amount of Ca has been proven to be beneficial for the structural stability of P2-type oxides due to its pillaring effect in the Na site, while Si occupying transition-metal sites is thought to be harmful. This work is expected to illuminate the actual influence of impurity elements on chalcopyrite for preparing durable cathode materials and pave the way for constructing low-cost SIBs based on natural ores.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.1.  Material Synthesis</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The chalcopyrite raw materials with target fineness and purity were obtained from natural chalcopyrite ores through a mineral-concentrating process consisting of crushing, ball-milling, and flotation. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39 ref42 ref43" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39 ref42 ref43" aria-label="reference 39, 42 and 43">(39,42,43)</a> The layered oxides were prepared by the solid-state reaction method. Typically, a certain amount of the chalcopyrite concentrate was pre-calcined at 850 °C in air for 10 h, cooled down to room temperature, and mixed thoroughly with stoichiometric Mn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> (99.5%) and Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> (2% excess for the compensation of Na evaporation) by ball-milling. The mixtures were then pressed into flakes under 20 MPa followed by calcining at 850 °C in air for 15 h. The controlled samples were prepared by the same method but replacing chalcopyrite with stoichiometric CuO and Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.2.  Material Characterizations</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The element ratios of the sample were confirmed by inductively coupled plasma-atomic emission spectroscopy (ICP-AES). The crystal structures were characterized by an X-ray diffractometer (XRD, Rigaku D/max 2550 VB +18 kW, Cu Kα radiation, Japan) and refined using the Rietveld method as implemented in the GSAS software package. The morphology and microstructure of the samples were investigated by a field-emission scanning electron microscope (FESEM, FEI Quanta 200, Japan) and transmission electron microscope (TEM, JEM-2100F, Japan). The X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) measurements were conducted with a spectrometer using Al Kα radiation.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6">2.3.  Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The working electrodes were prepared by mixing the active materials (70 wt %), super P (20 wt %), and polyvinylidene fluoride (PVdF, 10 wt %) using NMP as a dispersing agent. The slurry was then spread on Al foil and dried at 110 °C in a vacuum oven for 12 h. The cathode loading mass is approximately 1.5 to 2.0 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. Employing the sodium metal as the anode, Cegard 2400 membrane as the separator, and 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in EC:DEC (1:1) with 2 vol % fluorinated ethylene carbonates (FEC) as the electrolyte, the CR2016 coin-type half-cell was assembled in a glovebox filled with an Ar gas. The sodium ion full cells were assembled using P2- or O3-type layered oxides as the cathode and hard carbon as the anode in a CR2032 coin-type cell. The hard carbon was obtained adopting the method reported by Cao et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44 ref45" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref44 ref45" aria-label="reference 44 and 45">(44,45)</a> The hard-carbon electrodes were prepared by mixing the active materials (85 wt %), super P (10 wt %), and polyvinylidene fluoride (PVdF, 5 wt %) in an NMP solvent. The mass ratio of the cathode/anode materials was approximately 1:2. The specific capacity was calculated based on the weight of the cathode. The galvanostatic charge–discharge and cyclic voltammetry (CV) tests were carried out with an Arbin battery cycler (BT2000, U.S.A.), Solartron analytical instrument, and cyclic voltammetry (CV, MULTI AUTOLAB M204). The half-cells in target charged/discharged states were disassembled in the Ar-filled glovebox, washed with a DMF solvent, and dried under vacuum for ex situ XRD and XPS characterizations.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i7">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">As mentioned above, the chalcopyrite is one of the most common copper sulfide minerals in which the Fe and Cu elements are tetrahedrally coordinative with sulfur in the lattice. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46 ref47" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref46 ref47" aria-label="reference 46 and 47">(46,47)</a> The desulfuration process is found to be an essential step for obtaining the corresponding layered oxides because the sodium sulfates with high thermal stability would be produced preferentially when directly calcining the mixture of chalcopyrite, Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, and Mn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>, as evidenced by XRD patterns (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/suppl_file/am9b17952_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>). It is worth mentioning that the Mn element is also important for the formation of the expected layered oxides due to its function as a structure pillar and for charge balance. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a> Finally, the layered Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>MO<sub>2</sub> (M = Cu, Fe, Mn) materials are successfully obtained through a two-step solid-state reaction process, including precalcination of chalcopyrite in air and the following high-temperature reaction step. In order to reveal the effects of impurities, the elementary composition of a concentrated chalcopyrite sample (denoted as C1) is first analyzed using X-ray fluorescence spectroscopy (XRF). As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a, the chalcopyrite is actually composed of S (26.8%, atomic ratio), O (26.5%), Cu (16.4%), Fe (17.1%), Si (8.5%), Ca (3.6%), and other trace elements like Al and Mg. Notably, the main heteroatoms beyond theoretical composition are Si and Ca, and the total proportion of other trace elements is less than 1%. The O element may come from surface oxidation and impurities like SiO<sub>2</sub>. Through a typical concentrating process consisting of crushing, ball-milling, and flotation, the content of these impurities can be further reduced. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39 ref42 ref43" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39 ref42 ref43" aria-label="reference 39, 42 and 43">(39,42,43)</a> For comparison, two chalcopyrite samples with higher purity of 91.8 and 97.4% are also prepared and denoted as C2 and C3, respectively. XRF results (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/suppl_file/am9b17952_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>) suggest that the atomic ratios of Si in C2 and C3 are reduced to 6.6 and 2.5%, while those of Ca are 1.5 and 0.1%, respectively. This is also confirmed by XRD results since the characteristic peaks of SiO<sub>2</sub> gradually vanish from C1 to C3, as exhibited in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b. In addition, all three chalcopyrite samples are transformed into mixed phases of CuFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and CuO after precalcining (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b), denoted as PC1, PC2, and PC3, respectively. The FESEM images of abovementioned samples are exhibited in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/suppl_file/am9b17952_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>. Clearly, the average particle size of C1 is more than 20 μm, while that of C2 and C3 is much smaller (∼500 nm). The precalcined products almost retain the sizes of their precursors.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/asset/images/medium/am9b17952_0011.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/asset/images/large/am9b17952_0011.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) XRF result of selected chalcopyrite (C1) and (b) XRD patterns of chalcopyrite samples with different purities and c) corresponding precalcined products.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/asset/images/large/am9b17952_0011.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b17952&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">With the same stoichiometry, PC1, PC2, and PC3 are mixed with Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> and Mn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> then calcined at 850 °C in air. Very interestingly, the products exhibit significant differences in the phase structure. The oxides prepared with PC1 and PC2 can be identified as P2 types (denoted as P2-1 and P2-2, respectively), while the product obtained from PC3 is found to be an O3 type (denoted as O3-3), according to the XRD patterns presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b–d. As illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a, typical layered Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>TMO<sub>2</sub> (TM = transition metal) materials are usually prepared in P2 and O3 phases, depending on the surrounding Na environment and the number of unique oxide-layer packings. The letter O or P indicates the environment where Na is located (O for octahedral and P for prismatic), and the number indicates the unique sheets of edge-sharing TMO<sub>6</sub> octahedra within the unit cell. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a> Usually, the P2 phase is formed when <i>x</i> &lt; 0.8, while the O3 phase occurs when 0.8 &lt; <i>x</i> &lt; 1. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref50" aria-label="reference 50">(50)</a> Notably, the mass of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> is enough to ensure the formation of O3-type oxides. Therefore, it seems that the phase structures of the final product are greatly affected by the impurities in chalcopyrite. In this case, ICP-AES is employed to confirm the elementary compositions of the as-obtained layered oxides. As listed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/suppl_file/am9b17952_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>, the stoichiometry of Na in each product is retained approximately 1, which is in good accordance with the ratio of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> in the precursor. From P2-1 to O3-3, the proportions of Si and residual S are gradually decreased while those of Ca are almost constant. Considering the excessive Na sources, the products are washed with deionized (DI) water and analyzed with ICP-AES again (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/suppl_file/am9b17952_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S3</a>). It is clear that the elemental proportions of Cu, Fe, Mn, and Ca in P2-1, P2-2, and O3-3 remain stable even after washing. In contrast, the relative proportions of Na, Si, and S are obviously lower than before. From P2-1 to O3-3, the stoichiometry of Na is gradually increased, agreeing well with the relationship between Na content and phase structure. Note that the Na content in O3-3 is lower than the theoretical stoichiometry of an O3-type oxide. This may indicate a phase change induced by water, which will be discussed later. Another phenomenon is the loss of Si after washing, which suggests that soluble and thermostable silicates like sodium silicate may have been formed via the high-temperature reaction, hence consuming a certain amount of Na. This hypothesis is further evidenced by the ICP results (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/suppl_file/am9b17952_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S4</a>) of the filtrate, which show that a certain amount of Na, Si, and S has dissolved into the water whereas the other elements are not detected. More greatly, Ca and the remaining Si can be effectively doped into the crystal structure. Owing to similar ionic diameters (as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/suppl_file/am9b17952_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S5</a>) and electrostatic stability, the Ca<sup>2+</sup> ions may occupy the Na<sub>2</sub> sites (edge-sharing trigonal prism space), whereas the Si<sup>4+</sup> ions may take up the transition-metal sites. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51 ref52" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref51 ref52" aria-label="reference 51 and 52">(51,52)</a> Considering that high-temperature solid-state reactions are conducted in air, the S element most probably comes from the thermostable sulfates like Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> produced during the reactions. However, Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> is quite soluble in water. Therefore, the residual S in the washed samples is suggested to be doped into the crystal structure as well, which might occupy the O sites.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/asset/images/medium/am9b17952_0010.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/asset/images/large/am9b17952_0010.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) Schematic illustration of P2- and O3-type layered oxides. Letters A, B, and C refer to the different oxygen layers. Rietveld refinement patterns of XRD data for (b) P2-1, (c) P2-2, and (d) O3-3.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/asset/images/large/am9b17952_0010.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b17952&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Based on the conclusions above, the XRD data are successfully refined by the Rietveld method. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref53" aria-label="reference 53">(53)</a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b–d shows the XRD patterns and Rietveld refinement of P2-1, P2-2, and O3-3 samples. The diffraction lines of P2-1 and P2-2 are indexed to a hexagonal symmetry with a space group of <i>P</i>63/<i>mmc</i>, while those of O3-3 are well matched with a space group of <i>R</i>3̅<i>m</i>. According to the refined crystallographic data listed in <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">1</a>, the occupation of Na in the three samples is 0.76, 0.63, and 0.91. Thus, the formulas of P2-1, P2-2, and O3-3 are described as Na<sub>0.76</sub>Ca<sub>0.03</sub>[Cu<sub>0.21</sub>Fe<sub>0.16</sub>Mn<sub>0.60</sub>Si<sub>0.04</sub>]S<sub>0.07</sub>O<sub>2−ε</sub>, Na<sub>0.63</sub>Ca<sub>0.05</sub>[Cu<sub>0.20</sub>Fe<sub>0.19</sub>Mn<sub>0.59</sub>Si<sub>0.02</sub>]S<sub>0.07</sub>O<sub>2−η</sub>, and Na<sub>0.91</sub>Ca<sub>0.02</sub>[Cu<sub>0.24</sub>Fe<sub>0.20</sub>Mn<sub>0.56</sub>]S<sub>0.05</sub>O<sub>2−σ</sub> in which ε, η, and σ indicate the possible composition deviations. Comparing the lattice parameters and structural information of P2-1 [<i>a</i> = <i>b</i> = 2.90099(9) Å and <i>c</i> = 11.21821(5) Å] with those of P2-2 [<i>a</i> = <i>b</i> = 2.90565(4) Å and <i>c</i> = 11.17490(3) Å], a slight contraction of the unit cell is observed, which may be attributed to the increased proportion of Ca<sup>2+</sup> cations in the Na layers with stronger electrostatic attraction. The lattice parameters of O3-3, <i>a</i> = <i>b</i> = 2.93351(1) Å and <i>c</i> = 16.56598(3) Å, are also given by the structure refinement.</div><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl1"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 1. Summary of the Structure Parameters of P2-1, P2-2, and O3-3 Samples Determined from the Rietveld Refinement</div></div></div><div class="scrollable-table-wrap" style="display: block;"><table class="table "><colgroup><col align="left"><col align="left"><col align="char" char="."><col align="char" char="."><col align="char" char="."><col align="char" char="("><col align="char" char="("><col align="left"><col align="left"><col align="left"><col align="left"><col align="char" char="."><col align="char" char="("></colgroup><thead><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><th class="rowsep1 colsep0" colspan="7" scope="col" align="center">space group: <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub>/<i>mmc</i> (no. 194) (P2-1/P2-2)</th><th class="rowsep1 colsep0" colspan="6" scope="col" align="center">space group : <i>R</i>3̅<i>m</i> (no. 166) (O3-3)</th></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center">atom</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center">site</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char="."><i>x</i></th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char="."><i>y</i></th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char="."><i>z</i></th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char="(">occ.
