align="char" char=".">0.6667</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.3333</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.0949</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char="(">0.069(2)</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char="(">0.071(0 )</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">&nbsp;</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">&nbsp;</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">&nbsp;</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">&nbsp;</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">&nbsp;</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char="(">&nbsp;</td></tr></tbody></table></div></div><div class="NLM_p">The scanning electron microscope (SEM) and transmission electron microscope (TEM) images of the resulting samples images are displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>. The particle size of the P2-1 sample is found to be nonuniform in a range of 1–10 μm, while that of P2-2 and O3-3 are distributed uniformly around 2 μm, which are in good agreement with the features of the corresponding chalcopyrite precursors. Notably, the particles in the three samples are all well crystallized with a hexagonal shape, demonstrating the characteristic of a layered structure. The high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) images further present the layered structure of these materials where the interplanar distances between the neighboring lattice fringes are well defined as 0.56 nm (P2-1), 0.28 nm (P2-2), and 0.55 nm (O3-3), corresponding to the <i>d</i> spacing value of the (002) and (004) planes of the P2 phase and (003) plane of the O3 phase. Moreover, energy-dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) mapping (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/suppl_file/am9b17952_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S3–S5</a>) show that Na, Cu, Fe, Mn, Si, Ca, S, and O elements are uniformly distributed at the surface of as-obtained P2- and O3-type particles.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/asset/images/medium/am9b17952_0009.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/asset/images/large/am9b17952_0009.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Morphology of as-obtained materials. SEM images of (a1) P2-1, (b1) P2-2, and (c1) O3-3; TEM and HRTEM (inset) images of (a2, a3) P2-1, (b2, b3) P2-2, and (c2, c3) O3-3.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/asset/images/large/am9b17952_0009.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b17952&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To further confirm the impact of the impurity elements on the phase structure, a series of composition-simulating experiments are employed using high-purity oxides as precursors. Note that the ratios of Cu:Fe:Mn in P2-1, P2-2, and O3-3 are slightly different, the controlled samples with the same ratio of Cu:Fe:Mn corresponding to P2-1, P2-2, and O3-3 are prepared first, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a. With excessive Na sources, Na<sub>1–<i>x</i></sub>Cu<sub>0.21</sub>Fe<sub>0.17</sub>Mn<sub>0.62</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>1–<i>y</i></sub>Cu<sub>0.20</sub>Fe<sub>0.20</sub>Mn<sub>0.60</sub>O<sub>2</sub> both appear in the P3 phase, which is known as the low-temperature phase or the derivative of the O3 phase when sodium ions are partly extracted. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref54" aria-label="reference 54">(54)</a> In contrast, Na<sub>1–<i>z</i></sub>Cu<sub>0.24</sub>Fe<sub>0.19</sub>Mn<sub>0.57</sub>O<sub>2</sub> is found to be an O3 type, demonstrating the non-negligible influences on the Na content caused by the composition of transition-metal elements with different valences. Noteworthy, the O3-3 sample with the doped Ca<sup>2+</sup> ions shows higher crystallinity and phase purity than those of Na<sub>1–<i>z</i></sub>Cu<sub>0.24</sub>Fe<sub>0.19</sub>Mn<sub>0.57</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. It was also proved that the composition of transition-metal elements may not be the only reason for constraining the Na content in the lattice since the P2 phase was not formed. Then, the structure evolution when adding Ca, Si, and S sources (same stoichiometry with P2-2) in sequence is further investigated in detail. The resulted XRD patterns are presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b. Compared to the P3-type Na<sub>1–<i>y</i></sub>Cu<sub>0.20</sub>Fe<sub>0.20</sub>Mn<sub>0.60</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, a P2/O3 hybrid phase is formed after adding a small amount of CaO (Ca/Na = 0.07/1) into the precursor, which may be attributed to the lack of sodium in local areas. With the addition of SiO<sub>2</sub> (Si/Na = 0.07/1) and CuS (S/Na = 0.15/1), the characteristic peaks of the O3 phase gradually vanish, and a single P2 phase close to that of the P2-2 sample is finally obtained. Meanwhile, the (002) peaks are successively shifted to a lower angle, which refers to the decrease of Na content in the layers. Thus, it is concluded that the existence of Si, Ca, and S elements in precursors will certainly affect the phases of products by consuming the Na source, hence constricting the Na content inside the layers. In this case, it is proposed that the phase design can be realized by adjusting the amount of the Na source. As displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c, utilizing PC2 as a precursor, the pure O3-type oxide is successfully prepared through increasing the mass of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> by 1.5 times. Similarly, using PC3 as a precursor, the pure P2-type oxide is successfully prepared by decreasing the mass of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> by 0.7 times. The elemental compositions of O3-2 and P2-3 before and after washing are also confirmed by ICP-AES as listed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/suppl_file/am9b17952_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S6</a>. Furthermore, the impact of Ca and Si doping is verified through contrast experiments. As seen from <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d, compared to P2-Na<sub>0.7</sub>Cu<sub>0.2</sub>Fe<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, which is prepared with pure oxides (denoted as P2-pure), the (002) peaks of the Ca-doped samples are gradually shifted to a higher angle, indicating the contraction of the Na layer spacing that can be ascribed to the strong electrostatic attraction between Ca<sup>2+</sup> and neighboring O<sup>2–</sup> ions. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50 ref55" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref50 ref55" aria-label="reference 50 and 55">(50,55)</a> In contrast, the (002) peaks of the Si-doped samples have been moved to a lower angle, as plotted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>e. These two small but important facts will exert non-negligible influence on the electrochemical performance of the as-obtained samples. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref52" aria-label="reference 52">(52)</a></div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/asset/images/medium/am9b17952_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/asset/images/large/am9b17952_0008.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. XRD patterns of controlled samples obtained with the high-purity oxide precursors: (a) controlled samples with the same ratio of Cu:Fe:Mn corresponding to P2-1, P2-2, and O3-3 (for comparison, the XRD patterns of O3-3 and a reported O3-type sample are also given in the plot). (b) Structure evolution when adding Ca, Si, and S sources (the same stoichiometry with P2-2) in sequence. (c) XRD patterns of as-designed O3-2 and P2-3 samples. (d) (002) peaks of Ca-doped and (e) Si-doped P2-type oxides.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/asset/images/large/am9b17952_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b17952&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">As known, most layered oxides are instable against moisture and air since water molecules are easy to insert into Na layers and the CO<sub>2</sub> molecules may induce the formation of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> on the particle surface. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref56 ref57 ref58" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref56 ref57 ref58" aria-label="reference 56 to 58">(56−58)</a> For P2-type oxides, the insertion of H<sub>2</sub>O molecules or the de-intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> from the lattice usually lead to the expansion of the interlayer spacing, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a, while the impure phase like the O2-type stack may emerge when <i>x</i> &lt; 1/3. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref59 ref60" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref59 ref60" aria-label="reference 59 and 60">(59,60)</a> For the exposed O3-type oxides, the spontaneous extraction of Na always gives rise to the rapid transition to Na-deficient phases like the P3-type stack, as illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b. It is important to point out that these air-exposed layered oxides usually exhibit deteriorative electrochemical properties owing to residual H<sub>2</sub>O molecules or protons inside the lattice and inactive Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> layers on the surface, thus requiring huge costs on the material storage. As shown previously, Cu substitution is favorable to enhance the structural stability of Fe/Mn- or Ni/Mn-based oxides. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27 ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27 ref34" aria-label="reference 27 and 34">(27,34)</a> In this case, the stabilities of as-obtained samples against air and water are further investigated. As exhibited in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c, the P2-1 sample seems to be not stable enough against air and water. Although no phase transition is observed, the (002) peaks have been shifted toward a lower angle after being exposed to air or soaked in water, showing the possible intercalation of water molecules or deintercalation of Na ions. Notably, the same phenomenon is detected in the test on P2-3 (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>e) and P2-pure (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/suppl_file/am9b17952_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>). In contrast, the P2-2 (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d) shows significant stabilities both in air and in water as no change of the diffraction peaks is observed. Considering the small differences of impurity elements among these samples, it is proposed that the content of Si or Ca inside the lattice may have critical effects on the structural stability of the materials. This speculation is then evidenced by the same soaking tests on Si-doped and Ca-doped samples prepared with chemical reagents. As plotted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a–d, the P2-type Na<sub>0.6</sub>Ca<sub>0.05</sub>Cu<sub>0.2</sub>Fe<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (P2-Ca<sub>0.05</sub>) shows an outstanding stability against water, while Na<sub>0.65</sub>Ca<sub>0.025</sub>Cu<sub>0.2</sub>Fe<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (P2-Ca<sub>0.025</sub>) and the Si-doped samples (P2-Si<sub>0.02</sub> and P2-Si<sub>0.04</sub>) experience structure changes after soaking in water, demonstrating that a certain amount of Ca ions occupying Na sites are beneficial to enhancement of structural stability. For O3-type samples, although O3-3 goes through a slight transition to a P3 phase after soaking in water, it was proven to be stable in air as exhibited in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>e. Noteworthy, when the Na<sup>+</sup> ions are intercalated back into the lattice by electrochemical discharging treatment, the characteristic diffraction peaks of the P3 phase disappear and the pure O3 phase is recovered back again (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>f), indicating that the phase change of O3-3 in water is reversible. In addition, the other O3-type samples prepared with C2 or pure oxides (denoted as O3-2 or O3-pure, respectively) are also found to be instable in water (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/suppl_file/am9b17952_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>).</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/asset/images/medium/am9b17952_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/asset/images/large/am9b17952_0007.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Schematic illustration of the structure evolution of (a) P2- and (b) O3-type layered oxides when exposed in air or water. XRD patterns of (c) P2-1, (d) P2-2, and (e) P2-3 in the pristine, exposed, and soaked states. Insets correspond to (002) or (003) peaks.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/asset/images/large/am9b17952_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b17952&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/asset/images/medium/am9b17952_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/asset/images/large/am9b17952_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. XRD patterns of (a) P2-Ca<sub>0.025</sub>, (b) P2-Ca<sub>0.05</sub>, (c) P2-Si<sub>0.02</sub>, (d) P2-Si<sub>0.04</sub>, and (e) O3-3 before and after soaking in water. (f) XRD pattern comparison of O3-3 in pristine, soaked, and discharged (to 2.0 V) states.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/asset/images/large/am9b17952_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b17952&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical performance of the P2-1, P2-2, and O3-3 electrodes are first evaluated in sodium half-cells at room temperature. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>a–c shows the cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of P2-1, P2-2, and O3-3 electrodes, respectively, in a voltage range of 2.2–4.2 V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na at a scanning rate of 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. For P2-1, the main oxidation/reduction peaks located at 4.07 and 3.98 V should be contributed by the redox couple Cu<sup>2+</sup>/Cu<sup>3+</sup>, while the oxidation/reduction peaks appearing at 3.91/3.78 and 3.72/3.60 V correspond to the redox couple Fe<sup>3+</sup>/Fe<sup>4+</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref33 ref61 ref62" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16 ref33 ref61 ref62" aria-label="reference 16, 33, 61 and 62">(16,33,61,62)</a> The other conjugated peaks between 2.2 and 3.0 V can be ascribed to a small quantity of redox reactions of Mn<sup>3+</sup>/<sup>4+</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref63" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref63" aria-label="reference 63">(63)</a> Notably, unlike the sharp and complex CV peaks of typical Ni-, Co-, or Mn-based oxides, which are attributed to Na<sup>+</sup>/vacancy ordering or multiple phase transitions, the CV peaks of Cu–Fe–Mn-based materials are smoother, indicating better structural stability during electrochemical reactions. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27 ref64 ref65 ref66" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27 ref64 ref65 ref66" aria-label="reference 27 and 64 to 66">(27,64−66)</a> Compared to P2-1, the CV peaks of P2-2 are not obvious, but it is clear that the peaks of Cu<sup>2+</sup>/Cu<sup>3+</sup> and Fe<sup>3+</sup>/Fe<sup>4+</sup> are enhanced, suggesting an increased contribution to the capacity from the high-voltage part. For O3-3, the oxidation/reduction peaks appear at around 4.0/3.5 V, 3.6/3.2 V, and 3.2/2.9 V, which could be attributed to Cu<sup>2+</sup>/Cu<sup>3+</sup> and Fe<sup>3+</sup>/Fe<sup>4+</sup> couples. Also, the oxidation peak at 2.6 V corresponds to a slight transfer from Mn<sup>3+</sup> to Mn<sup>4+</sup>. Compared to the P2-type samples, here, the shift of these peaks may have resulted from the polarization associated with the different Na ion diffusion coefficients between the P2 and O3 phase. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref67" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref67" aria-label="reference 67">(67)</a> The first and second galvanostatic charge–discharge profiles of the as-prepared electrodes are exhibited in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>d–f. At the rate of 0.1 C (1 C = 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>), the initial discharge capacities of P2-1, P2-2, and O3-3 electrodes are 69.8, 77.5, and 107.6 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with Coulombic efficiencies of 94.0, 87.7, and 91.6%, respectively. Clearly, the O3-type sample delivers higher specific capacity than that of P2-type ones due to the higher sodium content. The profiles of P2-type electrodes can be divided into two sloping regions, suggesting a solid-solution reaction process instead of phase transition. Especially, compared with P2-1, the discharge profiles of P2-2 exhibit a longer sloping region above the voltage of 3.2 V, which is well consistent with the CV curves. As for O3-3, a small voltage plateau is detected at approximately 3.1 V, which indicates a phase transition. This point is also evidenced by the weak but sharp peaks around 3.1 V in the CV curves. Besides, the average discharge voltages of these three electrodes are 3.1, 3.3, and 3.2 V.</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/asset/images/medium/am9b17952_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/asset/images/large/am9b17952_0005.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. (a–c) Cyclic voltammetry curves (0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup>) and (d–f) galvanostatic charge–discharge curves (0.1 C) of P2-1, P2-2, and O3-3 electrodes, respectively, in a voltage range of 2.2–4.2 V vs Na+/Na.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/asset/images/large/am9b17952_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b17952&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The ex situ XRD experiments are further employed to investigate the structure evolution of as prepared P2- and O3-type cathode materials during the first charge–discharge process. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>a shows the XRD patterns of the P2-2 electrode in selected charge or discharge states. The half-cell is cycled at a 0.1 C rate in the voltage range of 2.2–4.2 V. It is found that the (002) and (004) peaks of the P2 phase are shifted toward the low-angle position, while the (100) and (102) are toward the opposite direction during the charging process. The reverse changes are observed in the subsequent discharge process. Most importantly, no new peak beyond the P2 structure is detected through the whole evolution, indicating a solid-solution reaction mechanism along with sodium extraction/insertion.</div><figure id="fig8" data-index="8" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 8</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/asset/images/medium/am9b17952_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/asset/images/large/am9b17952_0004.jpeg" id="gr8" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 8. Ex situ XRD patterns collected at selected voltages during the first charge–discharge process of the (a) P2-2 and (b) O3-3 electrodes cycled between 2.2 and 4.2 V under a current rate of 0.1 C. Black stars represent peaks from the Al foil. The curves displayed on the right side are the corresponding galvanostatic charge–discharge profiles.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/asset/images/large/am9b17952_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b17952&amp;id=fig8"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>b exhibits the XRD pattern evolution of the O3-3 electrodes. When the electrode is charged back to 3.3 V, a new peak appears at approximately 45.2°, which is assigned to the hexagonal P3 phase, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref68" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref68" aria-label="reference 68">(68)</a> suggesting that a phase is transformed from O3 to P3 when the Na is deintercalated. Then, the XRD patterns collected at 3.6 and 3.9 V show a continuous peak shift and no new peak appearing, indicating a solid-solution reaction in this region. However, when the electrode is fully charged to 4.2 V, the P3 phase is transformed to another new hexagonal O3 phase denoted as the O′3 phase as the (003) and (006) peaks shift back to a higher angle. During the subsequent discharge process, the XRD patterns exhibit a completely opposite evolution compared with the charge process, indicating a highly reversible phase transition process of O3-type cathode materials along with Na deintercalation and intercalation. Additionally, the valence states of Cu, Fe, and Mn elements during the charge process are further measured by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS). However, the obtained signals are found to be too weak to distinguish, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/suppl_file/am9b17952_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>, which may be attributed to surface contamination. Hence, the more precise methods like X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS) are needed to reveal the valence evolution of the transition metal elements. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref69 ref70" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref69 ref70" aria-label="reference 69 and 70">(69,70)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The rate performances of P2-1, P2-2, and O3-3 electrodes are exhibited in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">9</a>a–c, respectively. Charged/discharged at various rates from 0.1 to 5 C, the reversible capacities of the P2-2 electrode are 78, 76, 73, 72, 67, and 56 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at constant current rates of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, and 5C, respectively. When the current rate is reduced back to 0.1 C, a reversible capacity of 77 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> could be recovered, demonstrating a good rate capability. In contrast, the capacities of the P2-1 electrode obtained at the same current rates are lower than those of P2-2, which may be ascribed to the larger particle size and corresponding longer Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion pathway of P2-1. With the high sodium content, the O3-3 electrode delivered high capacities at low current rates but suffers relatively large capacity fading along the current rates. At a high current rate of 5 C, a reversible capacity of 58 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> remains, which is 54% of the initial value and much lower than that of P2-2 (72%). This further suggests the better kinetics of P2-type oxides than that of the O3-type due to the different Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">9</a>d,e presents the cycling performances of these three electrodes at a current rate of 1 C. After 100 cycles, the discharge capacities of P2-1, P2-2, and O3-3 electrodes remain to be 51.1, 62.7, and 78.7 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with capacity retention ratios of 90.9, 90.3, and 94.9%, respectively. Especially, at a high current rate of 2 C, the capacity of the O3-3 electrode remains above 84% after 200 cycles. Noteworthy, these performances are quite comparable to the Cu–Fe–Mn-based materials reported before.</div><figure id="fig9" data-index="9" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 9</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/asset/images/medium/am9b17952_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/asset/images/large/am9b17952_0003.jpeg" id="gr9" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 9. (a–c) Rate performance plots (0.1 to 5 C) and (d–f) cyclic performance plots (1 C) of P2-1, P2-2, and O3-3 electrodes, respectively. (g) Long cycling performance of the O3-3 electrode (2 C).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/asset/images/large/am9b17952_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b17952&amp;id=fig9"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Furthermore, the rate and cycling performance of as-obtained samples after soaking in water are also determined in sodium half-cells, as displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">10</a>. Notably, the rate capabilities of the three materials are all enhanced, which can be attributed to the improvement on the conductivity as the inactive substances like Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>, and Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> are removed. However, in spite of the increased capacities at the initial stage of cycling, the capacity retentions of P2-1, P2-2, and O3-3 electrodes are reduced to 76.7, 84.1, and 75.6, respectively, further demonstrating the good structural stability of the P2-2 sample against water. In addition, the stability of the O3-3 sample against air is evidenced by the test upon the materials after exposure for 2 months. As exhibited in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/suppl_file/am9b17952_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>, there is almost no decay in the performance. In addition, the rate and cycling performance of the controlled samples P2-pure, P2-3, and O3-2 are determined as exhibited in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/suppl_file/am9b17952_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S10–S12</a>. Comparing all the electrochemical results above, it is proposed that P2-type oxides are more tolerant with the impurity elements than O3-type ones. In particular, among the as-obtained P2-type materials (P2-1, P2-2, P2-3, and P2-pure), the P2-2 sample with relatively more Ca and less Si atoms remaining in the lattice exhibits the best rate and cycling performance, suggesting that the self-doped Ca ions in Na sites help enhance the stability of the P2-type structure by suppressing Na<sup>+</sup>–vacancy ordering. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref51" aria-label="reference 51">(51)</a> What is more, when comparing the initial charge–discharge profiles of P2-2 and P2-pure as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/suppl_file/am9b17952_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13</a>, the lower polarization potential of P2-2 also demonstrates the positive roles of Ca<sup>2+</sup> in Na layers. In contrast, the worse rate performance of P2-Si<sub>0.04</sub> than that of P2-pure (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/suppl_file/am9b17952_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S14</a>) confirms the negative effects of Si doping, possibly owing to the migration of TM ions into the Na layer. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a> Certainly, it is speculated that further increasing the Ca<sup>2+</sup> content may cause an unfavorable change of status, and there must be a critical value for the content of Ca<sup>2+</sup> that can meet the balance between kinetics and structural stability. Besides, for O3-type materials, less impurities seem to be more favorable since the rate capability and cycling stability of O3-3 are all better than those of O3-2.</div><figure id="fig10" data-index="10" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 10</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/asset/images/medium/am9b17952_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/asset/images/large/am9b17952_0002.jpeg" id="gr10" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 10. (a–c) Galvanostatic charge–discharge curves at (0.1 C), (d–f) rate performance plots (0.1 C to 5 C), and (g–i) cyclic performance plots (1 C) of P2-1, P2-2, and O3-3 materials after soaking in water, respectively.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/asset/images/large/am9b17952_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b17952&amp;id=fig10"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">Using as-obtained P2-2 and O3-3 materials as the cathode and hard carbon (HC) as the anode, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref71" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref71" aria-label="reference 71">(71)</a> the sodium-ion full batteries are further assembled in CR2032-type coin cells. The electrochemical results of the hard carbon electrode, P2-2/HC full cell, and O3-3/HC full cell are plotted in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/suppl_file/am9b17952_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S15–S17</a>, respectively. Cycled at a current rate of 0.2 C within the voltage range of 1.0–4.0 V, both P2-2/HC and O3-3/HC exhibited good cycling stability at least in 20 cycles. The capacities (based on the cathode) of P2-2/HC are obviously higher than those of O3-3/HC. However, noteworthy, the P2-2 cathode only contributed about 2/3 of the Na<sup>+</sup>, while the extra Na<sup>+</sup> may came from the electrolyte or pre-sodiated HC, showing the main drawbacks of P2-type cathodes for the practical applications. Overall, these results prove the considerable practical potential of as-obtained cathode materials derived from the natural chalcopyrite ores.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i9">4.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, for the first time, we propose a novel strategy to prepare Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>MO<sub>2</sub> (M = Cu, Fe, Mn) layered cathodes by utilizing chalcopyrite as the main raw material. The impact of the impurity elements (including S, Si, and Ca) from the raw materials upon the phase structure of final products is clearly revealed by a combination of XRD, Rietveld refinement, ICP, and composition-simulating experiments. On the one hand, the residual S and Si elements may consume a certain amount of the Na source via the formation of soluble but thermostable sulfates or silicates, leading to a limitation of Na content in the lattice. On the other hand, residual Ca and a part of Si elements could be introduced into the lattice like a self-doping process, resulting in significant effects to the structural stability of as-obtained materials by modulating the layer spacing. Both the P2- and O3-type oxides derived from natural chalcopyrite have achieved excellent electrochemical performances, which are quite comparable to the same-type materials obtained with chemical reagents, according to the results of half- and full-cell tests. Notably, the P2-type products are found to be more tolerant with the impurity elements than the O3-type products. The existence of a certain amount of Ca that remained in Na sites even endows the P2-type electrode with better rate capability and cycling stability, while Si occupying transition-metal sites is thought to be harmful. Actually, these results have inspired a hierarchical utilizing strategy for the chalcopyrite raw materials with different purities. For example, the chalcopyrite with a relatively high impurity content is suitable for synthesizing P2-type products, while that with a low impurity content is more favorable for O3-type products. Then, the key points control the content, species, and uniformity of the impurity elements during the mineral-concentrating process. In spite of this, the strategy presented in this work still holds great potential for preparing durable cathode materials for SIBs compared to the traditional routes in an overall consideration of the cost, environment, and energy consumption.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i10">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.9b17952?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.9b17952</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">XRD patterns of other control samples; SEM images of chalcopyrite raw materials; Elemental mapping of P2-1, P2-2, and P2-3; XPS spectra of P2-2 and O3-3; rate and cycling performance of O3-3 exposed in air for 2 months, P2-pure, P2-3, O3-2, P2-Si<sub>0.04</sub>, and sodium ion full cells; and XRF and ICP results (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/suppl_file/am9b17952_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b17952/suppl_file/am9b17952_si_001.pdf">am9b17952_si_001.pdf (2.66 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.9b17952" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Thermal convection induced TiO2 microclews as superior electrode materials for lithium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhao, Lijiang; Wang, Shitong; Pan, Feng; Tang, Zilong; Zhang, Zhongtai; Zhong, Shengwen; Zhang, Junying</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">6</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">One great challenge of lithium-ion battery (LIB) commercialization is to achieve high rate capacity at large mass loading d.  Here, a thermal-convection hydrothermal method was newly designed and developed to synthesize TiO2 microclews (TiO2 MCs) for their use as LIB electrode materials.  The unique MC architecture could be immobilized on a carbon cloth collector uniformly to form a 3D flexible conductive network (TiO2 MCs@CC), which facilitates fast Li-ion and electron transport and promotes continuous lithiation reaction during the charge-discharge process.  With these merits, TiO2 MCs@CC with high loading d. (up to 4 mg cm-2) exhibits ultra-high rate performance (75 mA h g-1 at an extremely high c.d. of 4000 mA g-1), outstanding long-cycling life (74 mA h g-1 over 1000 cycles at a specific c.d. of 2000 mA g-1) and a high initial coulombic efficiency (ICE) of 82%.  The demonstrated advantages open up possibilities for fabricating ultrafast rechargeable LIBs with potential industrial applications.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref7" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1039/C8EE01419A"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gür, T.                         cas_cit8R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">8</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-ion batteries: present and future</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hwang, Jang-Yeon; Myung, Seung-Taek; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">46</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3529-3614</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CSRVBR</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0306-0012</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.  This will provide important insights into scientific and practical issues in the development of SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref9" class="refNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit9" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201503816"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hou, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Banks, C.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Here, the extremely simplified and productive synthesis procedure of CQDs and their deriv. 3D porous carbon frameworks are studied.                         cas_cit10R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">10</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Room-temperature stationary sodium-ion batteries for large-scale electric energy storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Pan, Huilin; Hu, Yong-Sheng; Chen, Liquan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">6</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Room-temp. stationary sodium-ion batteries have attracted great attention particularly in large-scale elec. energy storage applications for renewable energy and smart grid because of the huge abundant sodium resources and low cost.  In this article, a variety of electrode materials including cathodes and anodes as well as electrolytes for room-temp. stationary sodium-ion batteries are briefly reviewed.  We compare the difference in storage behavior between Na and Li in their analogous electrodes and summarize the sodium storage mechanisms in the available electrode materials.  This review also includes some new results from our group and our thoughts on developing new materials.  Some perspectives and directions on designing better materials for practical applications are pointed out based on knowledge from the literature and our experience.  Through this extensive literature review, the search for suitable electrode and electrolyte materials for stationary sodium-ion batteries is still challenging.  However, after intensive research efforts, we believe that low-cost, long-life and room-temp. sodium-ion batteries would be promising for applications in large-scale energy storage system in the near future.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref11" class="refNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit11" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201701130"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yin, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shi, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, C.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Cubic Perovskite Fluoride as Open Framework Cathode for Na-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Antimony represents a class of unique functional materials in sodium-ion batteries with high theor. capacity (660 mA h g-1).  The utilization of carbonaceous materials as a buffer layer has been considered an effective approach to alleviate rapid capacity fading.  Herein, the antimony/nitrogen-doping porous carbon (Sb/NPC) composite with polyaniline nanosheets as a carbon source has been successfully achieved.  In addn., our strategy involves three processes, a tunable org. polyreaction, a thermal annealing process, and a cost-effective redn. reaction.  The as-prepd.                         cas_cit13R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">13</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A comprehensive review of sodium layered oxides: powerful cathodes for Na-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Han, Man Huon; Gonzalo, Elena; Singh, Gurpreet; Rojo, Teofilo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The room temp. Na-ion secondary battery has been under focus lately due to its feasibility to compete against the already well-established Li-ion secondary battery.  Although there are many obstacles to overcome before the Na-ion battery becomes com. available, recent research discoveries corroborate that some of the cathode materials for the Na-ion battery have indeed indisputable advantages over its Li-ion counterparts.  In this publication, a comprehensive review of layered oxides (NaTMO2, TM = Ti, V, Cr, Mn, Fe, Co, Ni, and a mixt. of 2 or 3 elements) as a viable Na-ion battery cathode is presented.  Single TM systems are well characterized not only for their electrochem. performance but also for their structural transitions during the cycle.  Binary TM systems are investigated in order to address issues regarding low reversible capacity, capacity retention, operating voltage, and structural stability.  As a consequence, some materials already have reached an energy d. of 520 mW h g-1, which is comparable to that of LiFePO4.  Furthermore, some ternary TM systems retained more than 72% of their capacity along with over 99.7% Coulombic efficiency for 275 cycles.  The goal of this review is to present the development of Na layered oxide materials in the past as well as the state of the art today in order to emphasize the compatibility and durability of layered oxides as powerful candidates for Na-ion battery cathode materials.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref14"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref14" class="refNumLink">14</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit14" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201703137"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Delmas, C.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sodium and Sodium-Ion Batteries: 50 Years of Research</span>. <i>Adv.     </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium transition metal oxides with layered structures are attractive cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries due to their large theoretical specific capacities.  However, these layered oxides suffer from poor cyclability and low rate performance because of structural instability and sluggish electrode kinetics.  In the present work, we show the sodiation reaction of Mn3O4 to yield crystal water free NaMnO2-y-δ(OH)2y, a monoclinic polymorph of sodium birnessite bearing Na/Mn(OH)8 hexahedra and Na/MnO6 octahedra. In combination of the favorable nanosheet morphology, NaMn2-y-δ(OH)2y cathode delivers large specific capacity up to 211.9 mAh g(-1), excellent cycle performance (94.6% capacity retention after 1000 cycles), and outstanding rate capability (156.0 mAh g(-1) at 50 C).                         cas_cit23R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">23</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Non-Prussian Blue Structures and Magnetic Ordering of Na2MnII[MnII(CN)6] and Na2MnII[MnII(CN)6]·2H2O</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kareis, Christopher M.; Lapidus, Saul H.; Her, Jae-Hyuk; Stephens, Peter W.; Miller, Joel S.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the American Chemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">134</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">4</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2246-2254</span>CODEN:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The aq. reaction of MnII and NaCN gives the 3-dimensional Prussian blue analog (PBA) Na2Mn[Mn(CN)6]·2H2O (1·H2O), which under careful dehydration forms 1.  1·H2O is monoclinic [P21/n, a 10.66744(32), b 7.60223(23), c 7.40713(22) Å, β 92.4379(28)°], while 1 is rhombohedral [R‾3, a 6.6166(2), c 19.2585(6) Å], and both structures are atypical for PBAs, which are typically fcc.  Most notably, the av. ∠Mn-N-C angles are 165.3(3)° and 142.4(4)° for 1·H2O and 1, resp., which are significantly reduced from linearity.  This is attributed to the ionic nature of high-spin MnII accommodating a reduced ∠Mn-N-C to minimize void space.  Both 1 and 1·H2O magnetically order as ferrimagnets below their ordering temp., Tc, of 58 and 30 K, resp., as detd. from the av. of several independent methods.  1 And 1·H2O are hard magnets with 5 K coercive fields of 15,300 and 850 Oe, and remnant magnetizations of 9075 and 102 emu Oe/mol, resp.  These data along with previous Tc's reported for related materials reveal that Tc increases as the ∠Mn-N-C deviates further from linearity.  Hence, the bent cyanide bridges play a crucial role in the superexchange mechanism by increasing the coupling via shorter MnII···MnII sepns., and perhaps an enhanced overlap.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref24"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref24" class="refNumLink">24</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit24" data-doi="10.1039/C6TA02061E"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Han, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yin, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xie, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yin, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, C.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Iron-Based Fluorides of Tetragonal Tungsten Bronze Structure as Potential Cathodes for Na-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Room temp. Na-ion batteries (NIBs) are receiving increasing attention owing to their potential application in large-scale stationary energy storage.  An open framework strategy has been utilized to construct kinetically favorable Na-ion channels with increased size or dimension, and to alleviate the generation of staged phases and multiphase interfaces during cycling.  Here a novel open framework iron-based fluoride of tetragonal tungsten bronze (TTB) structure is reported as a potential cathode for NIBs.  This tunnel-type fluoride is prepd. by topotactically densifying a hexagonal tungsten bronze (HTB) framework with intrinsic corner-sharing FeF6 octahedral chains.  K-ions as more robust channel fillers than H2O mols. enable a convenient solid state reaction or mechanochem. synthesis of desired open frameworks moreover at a redn. state.  K-stuffed TTB fluoride achieves a favorable electrochem. K extn. with a first charge capacity as high as 125 mA h g-1 and the following reversible Na-storage with a discharge capacity of 100-150 mA h g-1.  Structural integrity and near-zero strain sodiation are also responsible for the good cyclability.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref25"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref25" class="refNumLink">25</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit25" data-doi="10.1039/C4TA06688J"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mortemard de Boisse, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cheng, J.-H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Carlier, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guignard, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pan, C.-J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bordère, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Filimonov, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Drathen, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Suard, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hwang, B.-J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wattiaux, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Delmas, C.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">O3-Na<sub>x</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> as a Positive Electrode Material for Na-Ion Batteries: Structural Evolutions and Redox Mechanisms Upon Na<sup>+</sup> (De)Intercalation</span>. <i>J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The electrochem. properties of the O3-type NaxMn1/3Fe2/3O2 (x = 0.77) phase used as pos. electrode material in Na batteries were studied in the 1.5-3.8 V, 1.5-4.0 V and 1.5-4.3 V ranges.  Cycling the Na cells in a wider voltage range do not induce a significant gain on long term cycling as the discharge capacities reached for the 3 expts. are identical after the 14th cycle.  The structural changes the material undergoes from 1.5 V (fully intercalated state) to 4.3 V were studied by operando in situ x-ray powder diffraction (XRPD) and were further characterized by ex situ synchrotron XRPD.  The low amt. of Mn3+ ions (≈33% of total Mn+ ions) is enough to induce a cooperative Jahn-Teller effect for all MO6 octahedra in the fully intercalated state.  Upon deintercalation the material exhibits several structural transitions: O'3 → O3 → P3.  Also, several residual phases are obsd. during the expt.  In particular, a small part of the O3 type is not transformed to P3 but is always involved in the electrochem. process.  To explain this behavior the hypothesis of an inhomogeneity, which is not detected by XRD, is suggested.  All phases converge into a poorly crystd. phase for x ≈ 0.15.  The short inter-slab distance of the resulting phase strongly suggests an octahedral environment for the Na+ ions.  X-ray absorption spectroscopy and 57Fe Moessbauer spectroscopy were used to confirm the activity of the Mn4+/Mn3+ and Fe4+/Fe3+ redox couples in the low and high voltage regions, resp.  57Fe Moessbauer spectroscopy also showed an increase of the disorder into the material upon deintercalation.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref26"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref26" class="refNumLink">26</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit26" data-doi="10.1149/2.058302jes"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Thorne, J.                         cas_cit27R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">27</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Designing Air-Stable O3-Type Cathode Materials by Combined Structure Modulation for Na-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yao, Hu-Rong; Wang, Peng-Fei; Gong, Yue; Zhang, Jienan; Yu, Xiqian; Gu, Lin; OuYang, Chuying; Yin, Ya-Xia; Hu, Enyuan; Yang, Xiao-Qing; Stavitski, Eli; Guo, Yu-Guo; Wan, Li-Jun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the American Chemical Society</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">As promising high-capacity cathode materials for Na-ion batteries, O3-type Na-based metal oxides (e.g., NaTMO2; TM = Co, Ni, Mn, Fe, etc.) always suffer from their poor air stability originating from the spontaneous extn. of Na and oxidn. of transition metals when exposed to air.  Herein, a combined structure modulation is proposed to tackle concurrently the two handicaps via reducing Na layers spacing and simultaneously increasing valence state of transition metals.  Guided by d. functional theory calcns., we demonstrate such a modulation can be subtly realized through Co-substitution of one kind of heteroatom with comparable electronegativity and another one with substantially different Fermi level, by adjusting the structure of NaNi0.5Mn0.5O2 via Cu/Ti codoping.  The as-obtained NaNi0.45Cu0.05Mn0.4Ti0.1O2 exhibits an increase of 20 times in stable air-exposure period and 9 times in capacity retention after 500 cycles, and even retains its structure and capacity after being soaked in water.  Such a simple and effective structure modulation reveals a new avenue for high-performance O3-type cathodes and pushes the large-scale industrialization of Na-ion batteries a decisive step forward.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref28"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref28" class="refNumLink">28</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit28" data-doi="10.1016/j.nanoen.2017.12.014"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ivey, D.                         cas_cit28R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">28</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Tailoring alternating heteroepitaxial nanostructures in Na-ion layered oxide cathodes via an in-situ composition modulation route</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Huang, Qun; Liu, Jiatu; Zhang, Li; Xu, Sheng; Chen, Libao; Wang, Peng; Ivey, Douglas G.; Wei, Weifeng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">44</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The major hurdle of room temp. sodium-ion batteries (NIBs) for large-scale energy storage applications lies in developing new electrode materials with higher energy/power densities and improved durability.  This work presents a novel Na-P3/Li2MnO3 layered composite cathode with an alternating heteroepitaxial nanostructure fabricated by an in-situ compn. modulation route.  XRD structural refinement, synchrotron XAS and aberration-cor. HAADF-/ABF-STEM were employed to understand the structure evolution accompanying Li substitution.  It is revealed that the in-situ formation of Li2MnO3 (Li-O'3) changes the crystallog. and chem. features of the neighboring Na-P3 layered matrix significantly and leads to the alternating Na-P3/Li-O'3 heteroepitaxial nanostructure.  This alternating heteroepitaxial nanostructure delivers an extremely high reversible capacity of ∼ 210 mAh g-1 between 1.5 and 4.5 V vs.  Na/Na+, much improved cycling stability and excellent electrode kinetics.  Its enhanced electrochem. performance can be ascribed to the effective suppression of the P3-P3'' phase transition and subsequent amorphization upon cycling to 4.5 V.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref29"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref29" class="refNumLink">29</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit29" data-doi="10.1038/s41467-017-00157-8"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Environmentally Stable Interface of Layered Oxide Cathodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Nat.                         cas_cit29R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">29</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Environmentally stable interface of layered oxide cathodes for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Guo Shaohua; Zhou Haoshen; Guo Shaohua; Li Qi; Zhou Haoshen; Liu Pan; Chen Mingwei; Liu Pan; Chen Mingwei</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature communications</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">8</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">135</span>
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn"></span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries are strategically pivotal to achieving large-scale energy storage.  Layered oxides, especially manganese-based oxides, are the most popular cathodes due to their high reversible capacity and use of earth-abundant elements.  However, less noticed is the fact that the interface of layered cathodes always suffers from atmospheric and electrochemical corrosion, leading to severely diminished electrochemical properties.  Herein, we demonstrate an environmentally stable interface via the superficial concentration of titanium, which not only overcomes the above limitations, but also presents unique surface chemical/electrochemical properties.  The results show that the atomic-scale interface is composed of spinel-like titanium (III) oxides, enhancing the structural/electrochemical stability and electronic/ionic conductivity.  Consequently, the interface-engineered electrode shows excellent cycling performance among all layered manganese-based cathodes, as well as high-energy density.  Our findings highlight the significance of a stable interface and, moreover, open opportunities for the design of well-tailored cathode materials for sodium storage.The interface of layered cathodes for sodium ion batteries is subject to atmospheric and electrochemical corrosions.  Here, the authors demonstrate an environmentally stable interface via titanium enriched surface reconstruction in a layered manganese-based oxide.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref30"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref30" class="refNumLink">30</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit30" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201701610"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Deng, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Luo, W.-B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yao, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, H.-K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dou, S.-X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">High Energy Density Sodium-Ion Battery with Industrially Feasible and Air-Stable O3-Type Layered Oxide Cathode</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit42R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">42</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Size-tunable natural mineral-molybdenite for lithium-ion batteries toward: enhanced storage capacity and quicken ions transferring</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jiang, Feng; Li, Sijie; Ge, Peng; Tang, Honghu; Khoso, Sultan A.; Zhang, Chenyang; Yang, Yue; Hou, Hongshuai; Hu, Yuehua; Sun, Wei; Ji, Xiaobo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Frontiers in Chemistry (Lausanne, Switzerland)</span>

The pursuing of natural electrode resources with high lithium-storage capability has triggered a plenty of activities.  Through the hydro-refining process of raw molybdenite ore, contg. crushing-grinding, flotation, exfoliation, and gradient centrifugation, 2D molybdenum disulfide (MoS2) with high purity is massively obtained.  The effective tailoring process further induce various sizes (5, 2, 1 and 90 nm) of sheets, accompanying with the increasing of active sites and defects.  Utilized as LIB anodes, size-tuning could serve crucial roles on the electrochem. properties.  Among them, MoS2-1μm delivers an initial charge capacity of 904 mAh g-1, reaching up to 1,337 mAh g-1 over 125 loops at 0.1 A g-1.  Even at 5.0 A g-1, a considerable capacity of 682 mAh g-1 is remained.  Detailedly analyzing kinetic origins reveals that size-controlling would bring about lowered charge transfer resistance and quicken ions diffusion.  The work is anticipated to shed light on the effect of different MoS2 sheet sizes on Li-capacity ability and provides a promising strategy for the com.-scale prodn. of natural mineral as high-capacity anodes.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref43"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref43" class="refNumLink">43</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit43" data-doi="10.1007/s12613-018-1595-6"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, J.-s.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, R.-q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Peng, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Y.-h.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Selective Depression Mechanism of Ferric Chromium Lignin Sulfonate for Chalcopyrite–Galena Flotation Separation</span>. <i>Int.                         cas_cit43R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">43</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Selective depression mechanism of ferric chromium lignin sulfonate for chalcopyrite-galena flotation separation</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yu, Jin-sheng; Liu, Run-qing; Wang, Li; Sun, Wei; Peng, Hong; Hu, Yue-hua</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">International Journal of Minerals, Metallurgy and Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Selective recovery of chalcopyrite-galena ore by flotation remains a challenging issue.  The development of highly efficient, low-cost, and environmentally friendly depressants for this flotation is necessary because most of available reagents (e.g., K2Cr2O4) are expensive and adversely affect the environment.  In this study, ferric chromium lignin sulfonate (FCLS), which is a waste-product from the paper and pulp industry, was introduced as a selective depressant for galena with Bu xanthate (BX) as a collector.  Results show that the residue recovery of Pb in Cu conc. was substantially reduced to 4.73% using FCLS compared with 10.71% using the common depressant K2Cr2O4. XPS measurements further suggested that FCLS enhanced the surface oxidn. of galena but prevented that of chalcopyrite.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Compared to chemosynthetic CuFeS2, natural chalcopyrite (CuFeS2) can be regarded as a promising anode material for exploring ultrafast and stable Li-ion batteries benefiting from it being firsthand, eco-friendly, and resource-rich.  Considering the nonuniform size distribution in it and the fact that homogeneous grain distributions can effectively restrain the aggregation of active materials, the engineering of size is deemed an effective strategy to achieve excellent Li-storage performances.  Herein, varisized natural CuFeS2 are obtained by facial mineral processing technol. and outstanding Li-storage performances are exhibited.  Along with the decreasing of size, the contribution of pseudocapacitive as well as the ion transfer rates are significantly boosted.  As expected, even at 1 A g-1, a remarkable capacity of 1009.7 mA h g-1 is displayed by the sample with the smallest size and most uniform distributions even after 500 cycles.  Furthermore, supported by the detailed anal. of in situ X-ray diffraction and kinetic features, a hybrid of multiple lithium-metal sulfur systems and the major origin of the enhanced capacity upon long cycles are confirmed.  Remarkably, this work is expected to increase the far-ranging applications of natural chalcopyrite as a firsthand anode material for lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) and inform the readers about the effects of particle size on Li-storage performances.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref48"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref48" class="refNumLink">48</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit48" data-doi="10.1038/ncomms7865"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hwang, J.-Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Oh, S.-M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Myung, S.-T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chung, K.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Delivery of high capacity with good retention is a challenge in developing cathodes for rechargeable sodium-ion batteries.                         cas_cit50R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">50</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Recent Advances and Prospects of Cathode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xiang, Xingde; Zhang, Kai; Chen, Jun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Na-ion batteries (SIBs) receive significant attention for electrochem. energy storage and conversion owing to their wide availability and the low cost of Na resources.  However, SIBs face challenges of low specific energy, short cycling life, and insufficient specific power, owing to the heavy mass and large radius of Na+ ions.  As an important component of SIBs, cathode materials have a significant effect on the SIB electrochem. performance.  The most recent advances and prospects of inorg. and org. cathode materials are summarized here.  Among current cathode materials, layered transition-metal oxides achieve high specific energies around 600 mW h g-1 owing to their high specific capacities of 180-220 mA h g-1 and their moderate operating potentials of 2.7-3.2 V (vs. Na+/Na).  Porous Na3V2(PO4)3/C nanomaterials exhibit excellent cycling performance with almost 100% retention over 1000 cycles owing to their robust structural framework.  Recent emerging cathode materials, such as amorphous NaFePO4 and pteridine derivs. show interesting electrochem. properties and attractive prospects for application in SIBs.  Future work should focus on strategies to enhance the overall performance of cathode materials in terms of specific energy, cycling life, and rate capability with cationic doping, anionic substitution, morphol. fabrication, and electrolyte matching.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref51"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref51" class="refNumLink">51</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit51" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2014.11.150"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Han, S.         </div><div class="casAbstract">NaxCayCoO2 (0.45 ≤ x ≤ 0.64, 0.02 ≤ y ≤ 0.10) is synthesized via a solid-state method and examd. as a cathode material to improve the cyclability of Na ion batteries (SIBs).  The substitution of one Ca2+ for 2 Na+ does not alter the P2-phase of undoped Na0.73CoO2, accompanying the continuous contraction of the unit cell vol. with Ca-doping.  The existence of Ca2+ in the prismatic sites between CoO6 slabs also affects the phase transition behaviors during charge-discharge (C-D).  The suppression of Na+-vacancy ordering, ascribed to the immobility of Ca2+ in NaxCayCoO2, leads to continuous changes of the cell voltages with no abrupt voltage drops during C-D.  The reduced cell dimension and the mitigation of distinctiveness between biphasic and solid-soln. domains during C-D improve the cyclability of NaxCayCoO2 in SIBs.  As an example, the negligible capacity fading in Na0.60Ca0.07CoO2 during 60 cycles (0.07 mA-h g-1 cycle-1) is contrasted with a substantial decrease in the reversible capacity of Na0.73CoO2 (0.56 mA-h g-1 cycle-1).  Also, a slower Na+ diffusion of NaxCayCoO2 in Na+-vacancy ordering regions, is sufficiently offset by a more rapid diffusion in all the other regions, which results in an improved rate performance.  The platform presented here (multi-valent cation substitution for Na+) could be used in other layered cathode materials to improve the electrochem. performance of SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref52"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref52" class="refNumLink">52</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit52" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201500956"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qian, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gu, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">New Insights into Improving Rate Performance of Lithium-Rich Cathode Material</span>. <i>Adv.
